        <div class="span9">
          <h1>Research &amp; Analysis</h1>
                      <div class="btn-row -page-actions">

  <section class="intro-wysiwyg">
    

      <p>U.S. Institute of Peace’s articles, reports, tools and other features provide policy analysis, research findings, and practitioner guides. These publications examine critical conflict issues at the center of the Institute’s work to prevent and resolve violent conflict.</p>

<p><em>The views expressed in these publications are those of the author(s).</em></p>

  <section class="page__main section -summary-spacing">
    
            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-0afb32de3c9ad4733009fe3b2d545e9cd30bcd3471c2822b882ab87fb901f11e">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2025/02/border-security-training-program-bstp" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2025-03/border-security-training-program-cover.jpg?itok=iCzyDmkp" alt="Border Security Training Program (BSTP)">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2025/02/border-security-training-program-bstp">Border Security Training Program (BSTP)</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, February 25, 2025</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta"></p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>USIP’s Border Security Training Program (BSTP) trains police officers from Kenya’s Border Police Unit (BPU) and General Service Unit (GSU) who are serving on al-Shabaab’s major transit corridor along the Kenya-Somalia border. The program, which began in 2017, is implemented in partnership with the U.S. Department of State’s Bureau of Counterterrorism and equips Kenyan police with the skills to better understand the civilian environment and cultivate relationships in order to more effectively achieve their mission and counter terrorist activity.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=6#filters">Fact Sheet</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/12/protecting-water-infrastructure-during-war" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-12/20241206_2023-yearend-ukraine-042_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=XxHjALdp" alt="Protecting Water Infrastructure During War">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/12/protecting-water-infrastructure-during-war">Protecting Water Infrastructure During War</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, December 6, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Cassandra Burns  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The weaponization of water resources is among the most devastating tools used in violent conflict. And while this tactic has been pervasive throughout history, it’s on the rise. State and non-state actors across the globe are increasingly exploiting the capture, control and outright destruction of water resources and related infrastructure to inflict indiscriminate human suffering and further their own strategic and military aims. To put this devastating trend into historical context: Out of all the recorded incidents targeting water infrastructure over the last 2,000 years, 41 percent have occurred since 2020.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/10/sahel-coup-regimes-split-ecowas-risks-instability-coastal-west-africa" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-10/20241024_burkina-faso-conflict-5_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=_GAi21rH" alt="Sahel Coup Regime’s Split from ECOWAS Risks Instability in Coastal West Africa ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/10/sahel-coup-regimes-split-ecowas-risks-instability-coastal-west-africa">Sahel Coup Regime’s Split from ECOWAS Risks Instability in Coastal West Africa </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 24, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Matthew Edds-Reitman; &nbsp;Rachel Joy Yeboah Boakye  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As policymakers monitor the spread of terrorist violence and warfare from the Sahel region, one broad threat to international and U.S. interests is West Africa’s 3.4 million people uprooted by the Sahel’s chaos. So far, over 110,000 have fled to four West African coastal states, a migration that signals new dangers to the region’s democracies, and to the Economic Community of West African States (ECOWAS), the multinational body that for decades has been central to promoting region-wide stability.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/10/indias-brics-balancing-act" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-10/20241017_modi-2018-brics-safrica_flickr_ac.jpg?itok=VQ99p524" alt="India’s BRICS Balancing Act">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/10/indias-brics-balancing-act">India’s BRICS Balancing Act</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 17, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Jagannath Panda  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Leaders of the BRICS (Brazil, Russia, India, China, and South Africa) will meet in Kazan, Russia from October 22-24 for the 16th BRICS Summit. BRICS will welcome its five new members (Egypt, Ethiopia, Iran, Saudi Arabia, and the United Arab Emirates), with at least 24 other prospective members joining the summit. As BRICS expands and looks to become a more influential player in world affairs, India faces the increasingly complicated task of continuing to deepen ties with the United States and play a leading role in Russian- and Chinese-dominant global forums, like BRICS.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=19171#filters">Question and Answer</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/10/whats-driving-bigger-brics-and-what-does-it-mean-us" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-10/20241017_brics-leaders-2023-safrica_indiamea_ac.jpg?itok=GZmxITul" alt="What’s Driving a Bigger BRICS and What Does it Mean for the U.S.?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/10/whats-driving-bigger-brics-and-what-does-it-mean-us">What’s Driving a Bigger BRICS and What Does it Mean for the U.S.?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 17, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Adam Gallagher; &nbsp;Andrew Cheatham  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Leaders from Brazil, Russia, India, China and South Africa (BRICS) will gather in Kazan, Russia, next week for the group’s annual summit, along with an expanded roster of members. This is the first BRICS summit since Egypt, Ethiopia, Iran, Saudi Arabia and the UAE joined earlier this year. Russia, which holds the BRICS presidency this year, has also invited over two dozen other countries, which have expressed interest in joining the group, for the first “BRICS+” summit. For President Vladimir Putin, hosting this summit is an opportunity to show that Western efforts to isolate Moscow for its illegal war on Ukraine have not been successful and that Russia has friends around the globe.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/09/without-sudans-warring-parties-geneva-whats-next-peace-talks" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-09/20240904_sudan-aid-blocked-5_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=NX9t7Zfo" alt="Without Sudan&#039;s Warring Parties in Geneva, What’s Next for Peace Talks?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/09/without-sudans-warring-parties-geneva-whats-next-peace-talks">Without Sudan&#039;s Warring Parties in Geneva, What’s Next for Peace Talks?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, September 4, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Alex Rondos; &nbsp;Hatim Badien; &nbsp;Jawhratelkmal Kanu  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>A U.S.-led peace initiative to end Sudan’s brutal civil war took place in Geneva over the last two weeks. But despite invitations and extensive international pressure, the Sudanese Armed Forces (SAF) declined to send a delegation to Switzerland altogether, while the paramilitary Rapid Support Forces (RSF) sent a delegation.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=19171#filters">Question and Answer</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/07/red-sea-crisis-goes-beyond-houthis" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-07/20240719_us-carrier-houthis-13_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=kFrLwAPf" alt="The Red Sea Crisis Goes Beyond the Houthis">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/07/red-sea-crisis-goes-beyond-houthis">The Red Sea Crisis Goes Beyond the Houthis</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, July 19, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ambassador Johnnie Carson; &nbsp;Alex Rondos; &nbsp;Susan Stigant; &nbsp;Michael Woldemariam  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The Red Sea is in crisis. At the center of the storm are Yemen’s Houthi rebels, who have unleashed a wave of attacks on ships traversing one of the world’s most pivotal maritime straits, putatively in support of Hamas’s war against Israel. The Houthi gambit in the Red Sea is imposing serious costs on global trade, as did the problem of Somali piracy, which reached its peak in 2010. The United States and some of its allies have stepped in to militarily suppress the threat, bombing Houthi positions inside Yemen. But although this episode is illustrative of the difficulties of Red Sea security, the crisis extends far beyond the trouble emanating from Yemen.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/07/famine-spreads-across-sudan-protecting-civilians-must-be-priority" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-07/20240718_sudan-conflict-4_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=kCP6jfn_" alt="As Famine Spreads Across Sudan, Protecting Civilians Must be a Priority ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/07/famine-spreads-across-sudan-protecting-civilians-must-be-priority">As Famine Spreads Across Sudan, Protecting Civilians Must be a Priority </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, July 18, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ilhan Dahir  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Since Sudan’s civil war erupted 15 months ago, civilians have disproportionately borne the brunt of the fighting. The African Union recently referred to the crisis in Sudan as an “unprecedented catastrophic humanitarian situation,” marked by the world’s largest displacement and hunger crises and significant civilian casualties.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/06/kenyas-crisis-shows-urgency-african-poverty-corruption-debt" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-06/20240627_virus-hunger-1_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=oYAkmC8T" alt="Kenya’s Crisis Shows the Urgency of African Poverty, Corruption, Debt">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/06/kenyas-crisis-shows-urgency-african-poverty-corruption-debt">Kenya’s Crisis Shows the Urgency of African Poverty, Corruption, Debt</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, June 27, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ambassador Johnnie Carson  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Kenya’s public protests and deadly violence over proposed tax increases this week highlight some of the country’s most serious challenges: high youth unemployment, deepening poverty and the glaring gap between living conditions for the country’s elite and its urban poor. This social crisis is exacerbated by severe corruption, a stifling foreign debt and a too-violent response by Kenyan police, who have a poor record in handling large demonstrations. Steps to calm this crisis are vital to preserve Kenya’s overall stability, its role as an East African trade hub — and its capacity to serve as a leader for peace, which the United States increasing has relied upon in Africa and elsewhere.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/05/kenya-key-us-partner-needs-help-foster-peace-africa-and-beyond" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-05/20240522_kenya-president-critics-3_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=E1c_6K12" alt="Kenya, a Key U.S. Partner, Needs Help to Foster Peace in Africa and Beyond">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/05/kenya-key-us-partner-needs-help-foster-peace-africa-and-beyond">Kenya, a Key U.S. Partner, Needs Help to Foster Peace in Africa and Beyond</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, May 22, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Alex Rondos  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>This week’s state visit to the United States by Kenya’s president — the first by an African head of state in over 15 years — is meant ceremonially to celebrate 60 years of formal U.S.-Kenyan relations. But Kenya’s current importance for America lies in its role as a valued partner, especially on the continent with the world’s fastest-growing population. President William Ruto can underscore both imperatives and opportunities for U.S. roles across the continent that will shape security and prosperity for next generations of Americans and Africans. Urgent issues include averting catastrophic famine in East Africa and activating economic investment to support stability and democratization.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

  <section class="page__main section -summary-spacing">
    
            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-9ad5ab1323526f7458d81f6baa60e4cf6bba5ebe283069535ce39b5070f46b8b">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/05/sahels-center-tension-rises-over-chads-disputed-election" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-05/20240515_chad-troops-2020_voa_ac.jpg?itok=L4ioZp9-" alt="At the Sahel’s Center, Tension Rises Over Chad’s Disputed Election">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/05/sahels-center-tension-rises-over-chads-disputed-election">At the Sahel’s Center, Tension Rises Over Chad’s Disputed Election</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, May 15, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Dr. Yamingué Bétinbaye; &nbsp;Kamel Fakhry; &nbsp;James Rupert  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>A disputed presidential election in Chad last week is making few global headlines, but poses new risks to African and international efforts to reverse the Sahel region’s spreading instability, conflict and human displacement. Chad is centered in the world’s largest belt of military rule: six nations across Africa that have suffered armed coups since 2020. Among them, Chad is the first to hold elections to restore civilian rule. But a string of setbacks to a fully credible vote has yielded a contested result that risks further domestic conflict and a narrowing of popular legitimacy for the next government, led by the incumbent transitional president, Mahamat Idriss Deby.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/04/peace-africa-boost-regional-blocs-west-africas-ecowas" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-04/20240419_ecowas-burkina-faso-2016_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=UoXV7WRo" alt="For Peace in Africa, Boost Regional Blocs — Like West Africa’s ECOWAS">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/04/peace-africa-boost-regional-blocs-west-africas-ecowas">For Peace in Africa, Boost Regional Blocs — Like West Africa’s ECOWAS</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, April 19, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As the United States and international partners work to stabilize Africa’s Sahel region — and to prevent its warfare, violent extremism and armed coups from metastasizing into Africa’s densely populous and strategic Atlantic coast — the West African multinational bloc, ECOWAS, has proven its value in resolving crises and promoting stability. Yet, as global security threats have evolved, ECOWAS, like other multinational bodies, needs updated capacities to meet new challenges. International democracies’ most effective initiative to support West Africa’s stability would be to partner with West Africans to strengthen their vital regional community. A similar strategy is valid across Africa.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/04/four-priorities-sudan-year-civil-war" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-04/20240418_ssudan-refugees-12_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=LNXGoOGD" alt="Four Priorities for Sudan a Year into the Civil War">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/04/four-priorities-sudan-year-civil-war">Four Priorities for Sudan a Year into the Civil War</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, April 18, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>This week marks a year of war in Sudan. A once promising revolution that led to the overthrow in 2019 of the country’s longtime dictator, Omar al-Bashir, has devolved into a devastating civil war. The fighting started over a dispute on how to incorporate the paramilitary Rapid Support Forces (RSF) into the country’s military, the Sudan Armed Forces (SAF). A year later as the conflict between the RSF and SAF grinds on, Sudan is experiencing the world’s worst displacement crisis and one of the world’s worst hunger crises in recent history.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/04/senegal-just-saved-its-democracy-helps-all-west-africa" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-04/20240404_senegal-president-1_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=ykBrxK78" alt="Senegal just saved its democracy. That helps all West Africa.">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/04/senegal-just-saved-its-democracy-helps-all-west-africa">Senegal just saved its democracy. That helps all West Africa.</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, April 4, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Senegal’s dramatic transfer of presidential power this week highlights that West Africa, routinely seen as a zone of democratic erosion and failure, includes an arc of resilient coastal democracies — from Senegal to Liberia, Ghana and Nigeria. The Senegalese people’s resolute reversal of last month’s constitutional crisis shows that U.S. and international efforts to counter violent extremism and military coups can reinforce a potent West African democratic constituency. Vital next steps include these: supporting Senegal’s democratic forces in shifting from “campaign mode” to inclusive governance; promoting economic investment to bolster youth employment and rule-of-law reforms; and energizing a West African democratic alliance against extremism and coups.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/02/peace-sahel-african-and-us-experts-urge-focused-partnership" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-02/20240222_2022-yearend-3_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=nTxcML1i" alt="For Peace in Sahel, African and U.S. Experts Urge Focused Partnership">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/02/peace-sahel-african-and-us-experts-urge-focused-partnership">For Peace in Sahel, African and U.S. Experts Urge Focused Partnership</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, February 22, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Katia Cavigelli; &nbsp;James Rupert  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The past month has sharpened a decade-old question for U.S. and international policymakers: How best, in 2024, to help stabilize what is now the world’s largest single zone of military rule and violent conflicts — Africa’s Sahel region? After three military-ruled Sahel states withdrew from the West African regional community in January, those juntas last week proclaimed an alliance aimed at resisting international pressures, including those for their return to elected civilian rule. Former U.S. and African officials yesterday urged what they called vital changes in U.S. and allied policies to prevent a dangerous spread of the Sahel’s crises.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/02/suddenly-senegal-new-risk-democracy-africa" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-02/20240208_senegal-president-sall-meps-2012_flickr-ac.jpg?itok=qwhAZkG6" alt="Suddenly, Senegal Is a New Risk for Democracy in Africa">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/02/suddenly-senegal-new-risk-democracy-africa">Suddenly, Senegal Is a New Risk for Democracy in Africa</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, February 8, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ambassador Johnnie Carson; &nbsp;Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The sudden actions by Senegal’s president to postpone this month’s presidential election by 10 months threaten to seriously undermine political stability and peace in a nation that has been a resilient democracy in West Africa, where multiple military coups d’état have occurred in recent years. This move poses risks of authoritarianism, violence and economic setbacks for Senegal’s 17 million people, and deeper regional insecurity. Friends of Senegal and democracy, in the United States, Africa and beyond, must unite behind the clear desire of Senegal’s people to maintain peaceful, freely elected democracy under its constitution.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2024/01/senior-study-group-sahel-final-report-and-recommendations" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2024-01/20240109_sahel-ssg-final-report_featured.jpg?itok=dAcWPNqu" alt="Senior Study Group for the Sahel: Final Report and Recommendations">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2024/01/senior-study-group-sahel-final-report-and-recommendations">Senior Study Group for the Sahel: Final Report and Recommendations</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, January 18, 2024</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Bipartisan Senior Study Group for the Sahel  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The United States has not traditionally viewed the Sahel as a region of vital interest, whether in terms of security or from an economic or business perspective. This has led to a pattern of reactive involvement shaped by the circumstances of specific events rather than proactive commitments. This pattern reveals the lack of a comprehensive strategy for the volatile Western Sahel region, which includes Burkina Faso, Chad, Mali, Mauritania, and Niger. In April 2022, President Joe Biden announced that the US government would advance the “U.S. Strategy to Prevent Conflict and Promote Stability” in coastal West Africa by prioritizing a partnership with Benin, Côte d’Ivoire, Ghana, Guinea, and Togo.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=7#filters">Report</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/12/taking-ethiopia-eritrea-tensions-seriously" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-12/20231215_eritrea-progress-18_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=B1thP8Pb" alt="Taking Ethiopia-Eritrea Tensions Seriously">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/12/taking-ethiopia-eritrea-tensions-seriously">Taking Ethiopia-Eritrea Tensions Seriously</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, December 15, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Michael Woldemariam  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The historically fraught relationship between Ethiopia and Eritrea is deteriorating once again. A seemingly momentous peace deal that brought the two sides together in 2018 now appears to have been a brief interlude in a longer arc of enduring rivalry. The sources of recent tension include Ethiopian Prime Minister Abiy Ahmed’s public posturing around sea access and dynamics seeded by the 2018 peace deal itself. Neither side can afford escalation, but open conflict remains a possibility and even outcomes well short of direct hostilities — perhaps a return to the “no war, no peace” situation of preceding decades — would be disastrous for the two nations and the broader region. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/12/chads-political-transition-inflection-point" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-01/20230110-place_de_la_nation5_tchad-wikicommons-ac.jpg?itok=97xjN8vN" alt="Chad’s Political Transition at an Inflection Point">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/12/chads-political-transition-inflection-point">Chad’s Political Transition at an Inflection Point</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, December 14, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Archibald Henry; &nbsp;Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>On December 17, Chadians will vote in a referendum to approve a new constitution for the country nearly three years into a protracted, at times turbulent political transition process. The constitutional referendum is expected to pave the way for President Mahamat Déby to run for president in the 2024 national elections after leading the country since 2021, and adjust Chad’s system of governance to be a unitary, non-federal state with increased decentralization and territorial autonomy.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/12/la-transition-politique-du-tchad-un-tournant-decisif" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-01/20230110-place_de_la_nation5_tchad-wikicommons-ac.jpg?itok=97xjN8vN" alt="La Transition politique du Tchad à un Tournant décisif ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/12/la-transition-politique-du-tchad-un-tournant-decisif">La Transition politique du Tchad à un Tournant décisif </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, December 14, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Archibald Henry; &nbsp;Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Le 17 décembre, les Tchadiens voteront lors d&#039;un référendum visant à approuver une nouvelle constitution pour le pays, près de trois ans après le début d&#039;un processus de transition politique prolongé, parfois agité. On s’attend à ce que le référendum constitutionnel ouvre la voie à la candidature du Président Mahamat Déby aux élections présidentielles de 2024, après avoir dirigé le pays depuis 2021, et un ajustement du système de gouvernance tchadien vers un État unitaire non-fédéral, avec en théorie une décentralisation et autonomie territoriale plus accrue.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

              <div class="Wallop-item">
          
  <div class="block -feature-bg" style="background-image: url(https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/block_contained/public/2025-06/20250615-philippines-us-5-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=UtrJmdQ8)">
  <article class="block__feature">

  <section class="page__main section -summary-spacing">
    
            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-e8c4fbb23dc1fa53d8336965a79332c85a5376b377ee42155cb12db65daaef4e">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/11/peace-sahel-can-us-work-algeria" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-11/20231129_mali-conflict_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=p84L7NrK" alt="For Peace in the Sahel, Can the U.S. Work with Algeria?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/11/peace-sahel-can-us-work-algeria">For Peace in the Sahel, Can the U.S. Work with Algeria?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, November 29, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Thomas M. Hill  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Amid the Sahel region’s crises — a continent-spanning web of communal and terrorist insurgencies and eight coups d’etat since 2020 — U.S. and European attention is focused elsewhere: on Russia’s invasion of Ukraine, China’s expanding global influence and the Israel-Palestine conflict. But an opportunity to promote stabilization in the Sahel, notably in Mali and Niger, could be U.S. collaboration with Algeria. Algeria shares borders with those violence-stricken states, and also the U.S. desire to help stabilize them and their Sahel neighbors. A first question for any joint U.S-Algerian engagement is whether the two countries’ visions for Sahel stability, particularly in Mali and Niger, are aligned or contradictory.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/11/civil-war-pushes-sudan-brink-humanitarian-disaster" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-11/20231122_ssudan-refugees-10_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=nou_Z5b1" alt="Civil War Pushes Sudan to the Brink of Humanitarian Disaster">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/11/civil-war-pushes-sudan-brink-humanitarian-disaster">Civil War Pushes Sudan to the Brink of Humanitarian Disaster</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, November 22, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ashish Kumar Sen  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Away from the headlines dominated by the wars in Gaza and Ukraine, a civil war between Sudan’s military and the paramilitary Rapid Support Forces (RSF) is pushing the country to the brink of a humanitarian catastrophe. As an allegedly genocidal RSF gains the upper hand, a U.N. official has warned that Sudan is “facing a convergence of a worsening humanitarian calamity and a catastrophic human rights crisis.”</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/10/after-six-months-civil-war-whats-state-play-sudan" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-10/20231019-sudan-conflict-refugees-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=S3hvFvcz" alt="After Six Months of Civil War, What’s the State of Play in Sudan?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/10/after-six-months-civil-war-whats-state-play-sudan">After Six Months of Civil War, What’s the State of Play in Sudan?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 19, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Alex Rondos  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>What started as clashes in Khartoum this April between the Sudan Armed Forces (SAF) and the paramilitary Rapid Support Forces (RSF) has devolved into a civil war. General Abdel Fattah al-Burhan, who leads the SAF, and his former deputy, the RSF’s General Mohamed Hamdan Dagalo, had worked together in toppling the Bashir regime in 2019 and orchestrating a military coup in 2021. But tensions over how the RSF would integrate into the SAF eventually led to fighting that has metastasized over the last six months. Caught in the crossfire are Sudanese civilians, who are experiencing a growing humanitarian crisis.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/10/northeast-india-manipurs-violence-echoes-sudans-darfur" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-10/20231012_india-ethnic-violence-2_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=JcA_ummD" alt="In Northeast India, Manipur’s Violence Echoes Sudan’s Darfur">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/10/northeast-india-manipurs-violence-echoes-sudans-darfur">In Northeast India, Manipur’s Violence Echoes Sudan’s Darfur</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 12, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Binalakshmi Nepram; &nbsp;Manal Taha; &nbsp;Kris Inman, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Rising violence this year threatens to deepen instability in India’s far northeastern region. Ominously, the bloodshed centered in India’s state of Manipur includes elements that were visible in early stages of the 20-year-old conflict in Sudan’s Darfur region. Darfur’s violence has killed or displaced millions of people and helped lead to this year’s civil war across Sudan. Tragically, both countries have seen these disparate conflicts intensify through widened opportunities for ill-governed ethnic militias and for hate speech. These evolutions have hardened local conflicts over land or water into more extreme, venomous warfare between ethnic or religious communities. Darfur’s example underscores the urgent need for responses in Manipur.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/10/can-algeria-help-niger-recover-its-army-coup" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-10/20231005_un-general-assembly-63_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=--PRNx36" alt="Can Algeria Help Niger Recover From Its Army Coup?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/10/can-algeria-help-niger-recover-its-army-coup">Can Algeria Help Niger Recover From Its Army Coup?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 5, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Kamissa Camara; &nbsp;Donna Charles  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Democracies and democracy advocates should welcome this week’s tenuously hopeful sign in Algeria’s announcement that the 10-week-old military junta in Niger has accepted Algiers’ offer to mediate in a transition to civilian, constitutional rule. Still, Algeria’s government and the junta left unclear the extent of any agreement on mediation, notably disagreeing on a basic element: the duration of a transition process. Algeria can bring significant strengths to a mediating role. In stepping forward from what most often has been a cautious posture in the region, Algeria creates an opportunity that international partners should seek to strengthen.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/08/why-brics-summit-could-be-big-deal" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-08/20230823_brics-leaders_flickr_ac.jpg?itok=jdU_V0J7" alt="Why the BRICS Summit Could Be a Big Deal">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/08/why-brics-summit-could-be-big-deal">Why the BRICS Summit Could Be a Big Deal</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, August 23, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Andrew Cheatham; &nbsp;Adam Gallagher  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The leaders of the so-called BRICS (Brazil, Russia, India, China and South Africa) are gathering in Johannesburg this week in what is likely to be pivotal meeting for the bloc’s trajectory. Russian President Vladimir Putin will not be attending due to an International Criminal Court warrant. But Moscow and Beijing will be pushing for the group’s expansion in a bid to strengthen the bloc as an alternative to the U.S.-led liberal international order. Over 40 countries have applied to join. But there is division within the five members. Brazil and India fear that expansion will dilute their influence and impact their nonaligned foreign policies.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/08/how-respond-nigers-coup-and-prevent-next-one" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-08/20230810-africa-turmoil-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=rXjhE9kq" alt="How to Respond to Niger’s Coup — and Prevent the Next One">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/08/how-respond-nigers-coup-and-prevent-next-one">How to Respond to Niger’s Coup — and Prevent the Next One</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 10, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Military officers in Niger are rushing to consolidate the power they seized 15 days ago, while West Africa’s elected governments and regional institutions are seeking ways to reverse the July 27 coup d’état. Niger’s coup seems a particular setback for democracy, completing a six-nation belt of military regimes across Africa’s Sahel region. Amid this uncertain power struggle, how can the world support Africans’ demonstrated demand for elected, democratic governance that meets their peoples’ needs? We should begin by hewing to several basic principles. One is to keep our responses to coups coherent — but not uniform. Niger’s coup is distinct; our response must be as well.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/08/west-africas-leaders-face-high-stakes-nigers-coup" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-08/20230810-asiwaju-bola-ahmed-tinubu-flickr-ac.jpg?itok=lRvKwAYk" alt="West Africa’s Leaders Face the High Stakes of Niger’s Coup">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/08/west-africas-leaders-face-high-stakes-nigers-coup">West Africa’s Leaders Face the High Stakes of Niger’s Coup</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 10, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ambassador Johnnie Carson  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Amid the global challenges to building international stability and peace, a crisis increasingly forcing itself to the foreground — even amid those of record human displacement, climate degradation and unresolved wars — is the plunge by six countries across Africa’s Sahel region into coups d’état and military rule. Eight coups since 2020 have left 150 million people under rule by their armed forces. The latest coup, in Niger, is drawing a heightened international response, and a key player, the 15-nation West African community, today that it will pursue a long campaign of economic and diplomatic pressure, and that it sees military force as a last resort.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/08/countering-coups-how-reverse-military-rule-across-sahel" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-08/20230803-sudan-govt-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=xmAVNpH5" alt="Countering Coups: How to Reverse Military Rule Across the Sahel">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/08/countering-coups-how-reverse-military-rule-across-sahel">Countering Coups: How to Reverse Military Rule Across the Sahel</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 3, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Kamissa Camara; &nbsp;Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Three years of coups around Africa’s Sahel region — eight of them in six nations, from Guinea on the Atlantic to Sudan on the Red Sea — leave many African and other policymakers frustrated over how to respond. The Sahel’s crises have uprooted more than 4 million people and could add millions more to our record levels of global human migration as Africa’s population grows and its climate destabilizes. Yet the pattern of coups and other evidence — notably from USIP’s Sahel fieldwork, counter-coup research and bipartisan analysis teams — offer guidelines for effective responses by African, U.S. and international policymakers.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/07/coup-niger-what-it-means-africa-us-and-partners" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-07/20230727-niger-coup-facebook-slider.jpg?itok=IUcF1D3R" alt="A Coup in Niger: What It Means for Africa, U.S. and Partners">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/07/coup-niger-what-it-means-africa-us-and-partners">A Coup in Niger: What It Means for Africa, U.S. and Partners</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, July 27, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Kamissa Camara  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>This morning’s coup d’etat in Niger only deepens the pattern of instability across Africa’s Sahel and damages what has been a rare process of fairly steady democracy building in the region. Niger’s democratically elected government has been a valued partner for African and international efforts to stabilize the Sahel against its web of insurgencies, extremist movements and military coups. Kamissa Camara, a former foreign minister of Niger’s neighbor, Mali, now an analyst on the region with USIP, says the coup underlines lessons already evident about how to improve international efforts to build democracy and peace.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-48a2624e7079139ddddc074b10cedaf34967a5c14aad0b9144920c9f6a077b58">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/07/chads-political-transition-might-be-its-last-shot-democracy-and-peace" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-07/20230706-les-forces-armees-tchadiennes-wikicommons-ac.jpg?itok=JLIe-NSG" alt="Chad’s Political Transition Might Be Its Last Shot for Democracy and Peace">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/07/chads-political-transition-might-be-its-last-shot-democracy-and-peace">Chad’s Political Transition Might Be Its Last Shot for Democracy and Peace</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, July 6, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Gondeu Ladiba  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The state of Chad’s political transition is not reassuring. Despite some signs of progress during the National Inclusive and Sovereign Dialogue (DNIS) last October, security forces engaged in violent crackdowns of protests shortly afterward — killing dozens of civilians. Meanwhile, now-President Mahamat Idriss Déby has moved to consolidate power ahead of elections planned for October 2024, leaving opposition and civic groups worried that Déby may not turn power over to a civilian government.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/06/earths-best-defenders-are-indigenous-they-pay-price-violence" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-06/20230608_nicaragua-violence-land-1_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=PJcXq4N2" alt="Earth’s best defenders are Indigenous. They pay a price: violence.">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/06/earths-best-defenders-are-indigenous-they-pay-price-violence">Earth’s best defenders are Indigenous. They pay a price: violence.</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, June 8, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Binalakshmi Nepram  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Little noted by the world, warfare in India’s northeastern state of Manipur this spring has killed hundreds of Indigenous people and uprooted more than 35,000 residents. This violence along the India-Myanmar border fits a global pattern, also little noted: For decades, some 80 percent of human conflicts have smoldered in the “biodiversity hotspots” where our planet’s flora and fauna are most threatened by battles for resources and wealth — and where Indigenous peoples suffer the violence while protecting humanity’s common ecological heritage. We should strengthen the world’s inadequate public attention and policies on this crisis, and 2023 offers a chance to do so. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/05/sudans-crisis-offers-new-lessons-building-peace-sahel" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-05/20230525_sudan-conflict-refugees-1_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=C9a_eNUx" alt="Sudan’s Crisis Offers New Lessons for Building Peace in the Sahel">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/05/sudans-crisis-offers-new-lessons-building-peace-sahel">Sudan’s Crisis Offers New Lessons for Building Peace in the Sahel</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 25, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.; &nbsp;Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Sudan’s five-week war has killed or wounded over 5,000 people, uprooted a million more — and reignited understandable frustrations over how U.S. and international policies can better prevent or respond to such upheavals. Amid heated policy debates, we should step back briefly to pinpoint lessons from this crisis that can improve our responses in Sudan and across the Sahel’s web of coups, insurgencies and extremism. Indeed, that task is urgent — both to address the complex evolutions in the region’s crises and to build support for smarter, steadier engagement, rather than a self-defeating retreat from the Sahel by global partners seeking democracy and stability.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/05/sudan-engage-civilians-now-not-later" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-05/20230518_sudan-unrest-7_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=PoJp4qZ_" alt="Sudan: Engage Civilians Now, Not Later">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/05/sudan-engage-civilians-now-not-later">Sudan: Engage Civilians Now, Not Later</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 18, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Over the last month, a series of cease-fires in Sudan have yielded minimal results. Fighting between the Sudan Armed Forces (SAF) and the Rapid Support Forces (RSF) has continued and even intensified in some places. While the capital Khartoum and areas surrounding key infrastructure remain the core battlegrounds, the clashes have spread into other parts of the country. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/05/what-sudan-needs-right-now" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-05/20230511_sudan-refugees-4_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=ddZVf8LG" alt="What Sudan Needs Right Now">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/05/what-sudan-needs-right-now">What Sudan Needs Right Now</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 11, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ambassador Alex Rondos  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The unthinkable is unfolding in Sudan. A humanitarian disaster is deepening, as the state is being torn apart. The spill over could impact East Africa and the broader region — already tens of thousands of Sudanese have fled. As we have seen with other conflicts in the region, it is likely that malign, foreign interests will seek to exploit the situation to advance their own interests. The risk of Somalia-like anarchy on the Red Sea is real if the current fighting continues and foreign support for the warring parties — the Sudanese Armed Forces (SAF) and the Rapid Support Forces (RSF) — continues to grow.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/05/new-nonaligned-movement-having-moment" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-05/20230504_un-general-assembly-28_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=f_wfr4EW" alt="The New Nonaligned Movement Is Having a Moment">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/05/new-nonaligned-movement-having-moment">The New Nonaligned Movement Is Having a Moment</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 4, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Andrew Cheatham  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Russia’s invasion of Ukraine has accentuated emerging geopolitical trends that have coincided with the rise and intensification of great power competition. The re-emergence of the nonaligned movement (NAM) as a geopolitical force is perhaps the most salient example. Indeed, this month’s edition of Foreign Affairs — a reliable barometer of key trends in international affairs — is dedicated to the “nonaligned world.” By definition, NAM states do not want to be forced to choose sides between the United States and/or Russia and China. But as we move into a multipolar era of accelerating great power competition, these states will find themselves caught between major powers.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/05/amid-sudans-chaos-youth-groups-work-peace" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-05/20230502_sudan-activists_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=2WrDdqJc" alt="Amid Sudan’s Chaos, Youth Groups Work for Peace">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/05/amid-sudans-chaos-youth-groups-work-peace">Amid Sudan’s Chaos, Youth Groups Work for Peace</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, May 2, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Rachel Palermo; &nbsp;Paula Porras Reyes  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Amid Sudan’s battle between security forces loyal to rival generals, young civil society leaders are working to stem the violence. These leaders are part of grassroots youth networks that have been central to Sudan’s five-year-old citizens’ movement for a transition from military rule to democratic civilian governance. Against the current violence, youth-led efforts are combating misinformation, providing humanitarian aid and organizing crowdfunding to secure food and medicine. As the international community presses combatants to end the conflict and safeguard civilians, it is crucial that they also support the youth-led civil society initiatives to stop the violence and address its causes.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/04/peace-poaching-and-pangolins-central-africa" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-04/20230425_china-virus-wildlife-3_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=TcPehIuN" alt="Peace, Poaching and Pangolins in Central Africa">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/04/peace-poaching-and-pangolins-central-africa">Peace, Poaching and Pangolins in Central Africa</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, April 25, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Alexia Tata  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The pangolin — also known as the scaly anteater — is a small, primarily nocturnal mammal that lives in parts of Africa and Asia. Reportedly the most trafficked animal in the world, the pangolin is desired for its scales and its meat, particularly in Southeast Asia. Data shows that a pangolin is poached every three to five minutes. But the demise of the poor pangolin, as well as other trafficked species, has implications beyond the obvious risks to biodiversity. As new research shows, the proceeds of wildlife trafficking also contribute to violent conflict in Central Africa. Researcher Alexia Tata discusses the findings of her research on this issue and its implications for peace and security in the region.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/04/whats-behind-fighting-sudan" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-04/20230420-sudan_open-source-un-ac.jpg?itok=xvQMuqel" alt="What’s Behind the Fighting in Sudan?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/04/whats-behind-fighting-sudan">What’s Behind the Fighting in Sudan?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, April 20, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Since Saturday, violent clashes between the Sudan Armed Forces (SAF) and the paramilitary Rapid Support Forces (RSF) have been raging in the capital Khartoum and in other strategic areas throughout the country. While it’s unclear who initiated the fighting, the situation brings the de-facto leader of Sudan, the SAF’s General Abdel Fattah al-Burhan, into direct confrontation with his deputy, the RSF’s General Mohamed Hamdan Dagalo, who is also referred to as “Hemetti.”</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/04/africa-heres-how-respond-russias-brutal-wagner-group" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-04/20230406_mali-mercenaries-2_nyt_ac.jpg?itok=5TleIZCh" alt="In Africa, Here’s How to Respond to Russia’s Brutal Wagner Group">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/04/africa-heres-how-respond-russias-brutal-wagner-group">In Africa, Here’s How to Respond to Russia’s Brutal Wagner Group</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, April 6, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The United States is rightly concerned at the growing role in Africa of Russia’s Wagner Group, which operates as an auxiliary of President Vladimir Putin’s authoritarian regime. Where African governments have asked Wagner for security assistance, the group deploys military, economic and political interventions that deepen violence, corruption and authoritarian governance. Wagner’s role disrupts Africans’ efforts to move their countries from violent conflict to stability. Yet many Western responses are ineffective, even playing into Kremlin messaging to Africa and the Global South. An effective alternative requires that we listen to Africans’ voices and respond based on our shared values.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>
