  <section class="page__main section -summary-spacing">
    
            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-f25f2ebb4f21531c9d4edb79b8aa21ba86221ea980e604e04d874882a36b40f0">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/04/mali-civil-society-takes-new-role-democratic-transition" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-04/20230405_wpve-mali-national-dialogue_smugmug_ac.jpg?itok=0KvuaM4l" alt="In Mali, Civil Society Takes on New Role in the Democratic Transition">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/04/mali-civil-society-takes-new-role-democratic-transition">In Mali, Civil Society Takes on New Role in the Democratic Transition</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, April 5, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Korotoumou Thera  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>In the decade leading up to Mali’s two military coups in 2020 and 2021, persistent governance grievances had left civilians without reliable public services while the military struggled to contain violent extremist groups. Hopes for a quick return to civilian rule post-coup have faded, as the country now nears three years under the rule of military leaders. While the transitional government has laid out a roadmap toward 2024 elections, there are growing concerns about the infrastructure capacity to carry out elections within that timeframe.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/04/au-mali-la-societe-civile-joue-un-nouveau-role-dans-la-transition-democratique" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-04/20230405_wpve-mali-national-dialogue_smugmug_ac.jpg?itok=0KvuaM4l" alt="Au Mali, la société civile joue un nouveau rôle dans la transition démocratique">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/04/au-mali-la-societe-civile-joue-un-nouveau-role-dans-la-transition-democratique">Au Mali, la société civile joue un nouveau rôle dans la transition démocratique</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, April 5, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Korotoumou Thera  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Au cours de la décennie qui a précédé les deux coups d&#039;État militaires de 2020 et 2021 au Mali, les griefs persistants en matière de gouvernance ont priver les civils de services publics fiables, pendant que l&#039;armée s’efforçait de contenir les groupes extrémistes violents. L&#039;espoir d&#039;un retour rapide à un régime civil après le coup d&#039;État s&#039;est estompé au fur et à mesure que le pays approche sa troisième année sous un régime militaire. En dépit des efforts du gouvernement de transition pour établir une feuille de route pour les élections de 2024, des préoccupations s’agrandissent par rapport à la capacité de l&#039;infrastructure nécessaire pour mener à bien le processus et s&#039;aligner sur le calendrier prévu.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/01/us-role-furthering-chads-democratic-transition" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-01/20230110-place_de_la_nation5_tchad-wikicommons-ac.jpg?itok=97xjN8vN" alt="The U.S. Role in Furthering Chad’s Democratic Transition">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/01/us-role-furthering-chads-democratic-transition">The U.S. Role in Furthering Chad’s Democratic Transition</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, January 10, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Yamingué Bétinbaye; &nbsp;Remadji Hoinathy; &nbsp;Allah-Kauis Neneck; &nbsp;Babouh Tih-Kwada Elisabeth   </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>When Chadian leader Mahamat Idriss Déby announced in October that the country’s transition period would be extended another 24 months, demonstrators took to the streets in protest, where they were met with violent repression from Chadian security forces. As the dust settled, Chadian authorities initially disclosed 50 people had been killed — but opposition groups and independent observers claim a much higher figure.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2023/01/le-role-des-etats-unis-dans-la-transition-democratique-du-tchad" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2023-01/20230110-place_de_la_nation5_tchad-wikicommons-ac.jpg?itok=97xjN8vN" alt="Le Rôle des États-Unis dans la Transition Démocratique du Tchad">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2023/01/le-role-des-etats-unis-dans-la-transition-democratique-du-tchad">Le Rôle des États-Unis dans la Transition Démocratique du Tchad</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, January 10, 2023</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Yamingué Bétinbaye; &nbsp; Remadji Hoinathy; &nbsp;Allah-Kauis Neneck; &nbsp;Babouh Tih-Kwada Elisabeth   </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Lorsque le dirigeant tchadien Mahamat Idriss Déby a annoncé en octobre que la période de transition du pays serait prolongée de 24 mois, les manifestants sont sortis dans les rues pour protester et ont été confrontés à une violente répression des forces de sécurité tchadiennes. Lorsque les choses se sont calmées, les autorités tchadiennes ont d&#039;abord révélé que 50 personnes avaient été tuées, mais les groupes d&#039;opposition et observateurs indépendants avancent un chiffre beaucoup plus élevé.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/10/another-coup-sahel-heres-way-halt-cycle" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-10/20221020-us-isis-2-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=VxX-1Oy6" alt="Another Coup in the Sahel: Here’s a Way to Halt This Cycle">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/10/another-coup-sahel-heres-way-halt-cycle">Another Coup in the Sahel: Here’s a Way to Halt This Cycle</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 20, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Sandrine Nama; &nbsp;Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>This month’s coup d’etat in Burkina Faso, the seventh in 26 months around the Sahel region, only extends the Sahel’s long agonies of failing governance, civil wars and violent extremism. Military-led intervention by France and other outside powers has failed to stem the widening destabilization of a landmass and population vastly bigger than those of the recent U.S. wars in Afghanistan or Iraq. U.S. and international security require a policy reset that addresses the Sahel crisis’ causes rather than its symptoms. In Burkina Faso and other states, this means supporting inclusive processes of national dialogue that can mobilize whole of the society to address those causes.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/10/ethiopias-civil-war-raging-how-can-it-get-track-toward-peace" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-10/20221018-ethiopia-massacre-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=TbxjTdm8" alt="Ethiopia’s civil war is raging. How can it get on track toward peace?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/10/ethiopias-civil-war-raging-how-can-it-get-track-toward-peace">Ethiopia’s civil war is raging. How can it get on track toward peace?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, October 18, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ambassador Johnnie Carson; &nbsp;Ambassador Alex Rondos  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>In August, the devastating conflict in northern Ethiopia resumed, effectively ending the March 2022 humanitarian truce between the Ethiopian federal government and Tigrayan forces, which many hoped would pave the way for a negotiated cease-fire and peace talks. This week, the African Union’s chairperson called for an immediate cease-fire and U.S. Secretary of State Antony Blinken also called on the parties to cease hostilities and participate in talks organized by the African Union. What comes next in Ethiopia will have major implications for its people, the strategically vital Red Sea arena and for U.S. interests in the region. Stepped up, senior-level U.S. engagement is direly needed to get Ethiopia on a path toward peace.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/10/chads-national-dialogue-concludes-amid-uncertainties-transition" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-10/20221012-mahamat_idriss_deby_en_boubou_blanc-wikicommons-ac.png?itok=3zs_aVSS" alt="Chad’s National Dialogue Concludes Amid Uncertainties for the Transition">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/10/chads-national-dialogue-concludes-amid-uncertainties-transition">Chad’s National Dialogue Concludes Amid Uncertainties for the Transition</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, October 12, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Yamingué Bétinbaye; &nbsp;Remadji Hoinathy; &nbsp;Babouh Tih-Kwada Elisabeth  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>When Chad’s longtime President Idriss Déby was killed in April 2021, a group of military officers led by Déby’s son, Mahamat Idriss Déby, took control of the country and outlined an 18-month transition back to civilian rule. A crucial part of the transitional timeline was the promise of an inclusive national dialogue, which would be tasked with forming a national consensus on constitutional reform, election plans and other contentious political issues. After numerous delays, the long-awaited national dialogue finally opened in the capital N&#039;Djamena in late August — just two months before the transition was slated to end.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/10/le-dialogue-national-au-tchad-se-termine-dans-un-contexte-dincertitudes-pour" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-10/20221012-mahamat_idriss_deby_en_boubou_blanc-wikicommons-ac.png?itok=3zs_aVSS" alt="Le Dialogue National au Tchad se Termine dans un Contexte d&#039;Incertitudes pour la Transition">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/10/le-dialogue-national-au-tchad-se-termine-dans-un-contexte-dincertitudes-pour">Le Dialogue National au Tchad se Termine dans un Contexte d&#039;Incertitudes pour la Transition</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, October 12, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Yamingué Bétinbaye; &nbsp;Remadji Hoinathy; &nbsp;Babouh Tih-Kwada Elisabeth  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Après que l’ancien président du Tchad, Idriss Déby, a été tué en avril 2021, un groupe d&#039;officiers militaires dirigé par le fils de Déby, Mahamat Idriss Déby, a pris le contrôle du pays et a défini une période de transition de 18 mois pour le retour à un régime civil. Un élément crucial du calendrier de transition était la promesse d&#039;un dialogue national inclusif, chargé de former un consensus national sur la réforme constitutionnelle, les plans d&#039;élection et d&#039;autres questions politiques litigieuses. Après de nombreux retards, le dialogue national tant attendu a finalement commencé dans la capitale N&#039;Djaména fin août – juste deux mois avant la fin prévue de la transition. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/10/42-months-how-does-sudans-democracy-movement-endure" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-10/20221006-sudan-unrest-1-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=KC6oM9dS" alt="42 Months on, How Does Sudan’s Democracy Movement Endure?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/10/42-months-how-does-sudans-democracy-movement-endure">42 Months on, How Does Sudan’s Democracy Movement Endure?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 6, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Jawhratelkmal Kanu; &nbsp;Jonathan Pinckney, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Three and a half years after Sudan’s military deposed the authoritarian ruler, Omar Bashir, in response to massive protests, the current military leadership and divisions among political factions are stalling a return to elected civilian government. This year has brought a deepening economic crisis and violent communal clashes — but also a new wave of nonviolent, grassroots campaigns for a return to democracy. As Sudanese democracy advocates and their international allies seek ways to press the military for that transition, all sides should note, and work to sustain, Sudan’s nonviolent civic action.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/09/whats-next-kenya-after-william-rutos-presidential-victory" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-09/20220915-kenya-president-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=ZKv6emoj" alt="What’s Next for Kenya After William Ruto’s Presidential Victory?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/09/whats-next-kenya-after-william-rutos-presidential-victory">What’s Next for Kenya After William Ruto’s Presidential Victory?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, September 15, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Chris Kwaja, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>William Ruto’s emergence as Kenya’s fifth president represents a paradigm shift in the country’s politics. Ruto’s campaign was comprised of a mass movement of workers, the jobless, peasants and other “hustlers” and sought to distance itself from the dynasties that have long run Kenya’s politics. While Ruto was born in a small rural village in the Rift Valley, his opponent, Raila Odinga, is a former prime minister and the son of the country’s first vice president. Marginalized Kenyans see Ruto as the personification of a transformational agenda that centers their plight, defining a contest between hustlers and dynasties. While Kenya faces a dire economic situation, Ruto’s biggest challenge may be overcoming the country’s legacy of ethnic politics and building national cohesion.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

  <div class="block -feature-bg" style="background-image: url(https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/block_contained/public/2025-06/20250615-philippines-us-5-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=UtrJmdQ8)">
  <article class="block__feature">

                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/08/three-early-lessons-kenyas-elections" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-08/20220818-kenya_election_16-nyt-ac.jpg?itok=AwiIw-T9" alt="Three Early Lessons from Kenya’s Elections">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/08/three-early-lessons-kenyas-elections">Three Early Lessons from Kenya’s Elections</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 18, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>On August 15, William Ruto was declared president-elect of Kenya, following a vote last week. His chief competitor, Raila Odinga, rejected the results and says he will go to court to seek their invalidation. So far, little evidence of electoral misconduct has been presented, with most observers suggesting the conduct of the polls improved compared to the last vote in 2017. As the country waits for the judicial process to unfold, here are three takeaways from this year’s Kenyan experience.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/08/senegals-war-torn-casamance-dialogue-builds-stability" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-08/20220817-04-meeting-usipstaff-ac.jpg?itok=ifTBjO7k" alt="In Senegal’s War-Torn Casamance, a Dialogue Builds Stability">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/08/senegals-war-torn-casamance-dialogue-builds-stability">In Senegal’s War-Torn Casamance, a Dialogue Builds Stability</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, August 17, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Boucar Baba Ndiaye  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Senegal, one of West Africa’s most stable countries, is a key partner in countering extremism, military coups and other violence in the Sahel and in coastal states. Yet Senegal’s democracy and stability face challenges, notably the 40-year insurgency in the Casamance region. As Senegal attempts political and security reforms to build peace there, a community dialogue process in one Casamance town is helping improve security. Local dialogues—among communities, government officials and security forces—offer an efficient method for Senegal and its partners to heal conflict, bolster Senegal’s stability and counter West Africa’s slippage toward violence.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/02/how-putins-invasion-ukraine-affects-rest-russian-foreign-policy" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-02/20220221-us-russia-intel-2-slider.jpg?itok=OMD7a1IC" alt="How Putin’s Invasion of Ukraine Affects the Rest of Russian Foreign Policy">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/02/how-putins-invasion-ukraine-affects-rest-russian-foreign-policy">How Putin’s Invasion of Ukraine Affects the Rest of Russian Foreign Policy</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, February 24, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Heather Ashby, Ph.D.; &nbsp;Jude Mutah, Ph.D.; &nbsp;Andrew Scobell, Ph.D.; &nbsp;Mona Yacoubian  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>USIP’s Heather Ashby, Jude Mutah, Andrew Scobell and Mona Yacoubian examine how the invasion of Ukraine might have shifted Moscow’s decision-making in other regions — such as Syria, the Sahel and China.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2022/02/sixth-coup-africa-west-needs-its-game" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2022-02/20220202-nyt-africa_coups_explainer-ac.jpg?itok=iQ63uljg" alt="A Sixth Coup in Africa? The West Needs to Up Its Game.">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2022/02/sixth-coup-africa-west-needs-its-game">A Sixth Coup in Africa? The West Needs to Up Its Game.</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, February 2, 2022</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The government of Guinea-Bissau says it survived an attempted coup d’état yesterday, just days after Burkina Faso suffered the fifth coup in nine months around the greater Sahel. These upheavals cement this African region as the most pronounced center of a global crisis: Poor and authoritarian governance is breeding extremism and transnational criminality, igniting violence and undermining efforts to build democracies. Following last year’s military power grabs in Chad, Mali, Guinea and Sudan, the new crises highlight widening risks to security — for the 135 million people of the Sahel region, and ultimately for Europe and the United States. They also point to changes needed in U.S. and international policies.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/08/mobilization-negotiation-and-transition-burkina-faso-french" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-03/sr_491-mobilization_negotiation_and_transition_in_burkina_faso-feature.jpg?itok=xCj5Y1Zq" alt="Mobilization, Negotiation, and Transition in Burkina Faso (French)">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/08/mobilization-negotiation-and-transition-burkina-faso-french">Mobilization, Negotiation, and Transition in Burkina Faso (French)</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 5, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Eloïse Bertrand  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>En octobre 2014, un soulèvement populaire massif a destitué le président de longue date du Burkina Faso, Blaise Compaoré, et conduit à une transition menée par des civils, laquelle a abouti à des élections libres et équitables en novembre 2015. Ce rapport illustre l’importance de la culture nationale du dialogue et du consensus et les avantages d’un vaste et solide réseau entre les groupes de négociation. Bien que la violence dans le pays ait augmenté depuis lors, les enseignements tirés de la transition au Burkina Faso peuvent être d’une aide précieuse pour guider les efforts de mobilisation populaire, les négociations et les perspectives de paix et de démocratie à long terme dans d’autres contextes.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=26#filters">Special Report</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/07/joseph-sany-turmoil-ethiopia-and-chinas-role-africa" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Joseph Sany on the Turmoil in Ethiopia and China’s Role in Africa">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/07/joseph-sany-turmoil-ethiopia-and-chinas-role-africa">Joseph Sany on the Turmoil in Ethiopia and China’s Role in Africa</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, July 21, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Sany, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As China steps up its engagement in Africa amid lagging vaccination rates and tensions in Ethiopia, USIP’s Joseph Sany says U.S. policy must avoid a narrow, competitive mindset: “[China] is doing what a major superpower does … [the United States] must address African interests, not impose American interests.”</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/07/five-steps-sustain-cease-fire-northern-ethiopia" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-07/20210702-nyt-ethiopia-conflict-ac.jpg?itok=LXGm6zE6" alt="Five Steps to Sustain a Cease-Fire in Northern Ethiopia">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/07/five-steps-sustain-cease-fire-northern-ethiopia">Five Steps to Sustain a Cease-Fire in Northern Ethiopia</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, July 2, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>After months of war and amid an intensifying humanitarian crisis in Ethiopia’s northern region of Tigray, the Ethiopian government unilaterally declared a “humanitarian cease-fire” on June 28. If Ethiopia wants this truce to end the war it needs to persuade Tigrayan forces, which have so far vowed to keep fighting, to agree to a deal. As a prelude to wider negotiations, the odds of ending the conflict would improve if confidence building measures are urgently undertaken and five key areas of a truce expanded. U.S. and international partners can promote these steps while recognizing that Ethiopians must own any cease-fire process.  </p>
  


        
    
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/06/why-ethiopias-2021-elections-matter" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-06/20210617-usip-addis-ababa-election-candidates-ac.jpg?itok=zEEClr2j" alt="Why Ethiopia’s 2021 Elections Matter">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/06/why-ethiopias-2021-elections-matter">Why Ethiopia’s 2021 Elections Matter</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, June 17, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee; &nbsp;Terrence Lyons  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Facing numerous technical difficulties, the National Election Board of Ethiopia (NEBE) delayed parliamentary elections from June 5 to June 21, postponing the vote for the second time. Some major opposition parties are boycotting, and no voting will take place in civil war hit Tigray or in several other areas facing insecurity. Elsewhere, deficiencies in election administration have meant voting has already been postponed in many constituencies, and some of the logistical arrangements to underpin the vote are still to be implemented. Although there are risks of electoral violence, any incidents are unlikely to be especially significant in a context of high levels of ongoing political violence. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/04/ethiopia-contemplating-elections-and-prospects-peaceful-reform" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-04/20210429-nyt-ethiopia_election_5-ac.jpg?itok=to2HiwWF" alt="Ethiopia: Contemplating Elections and the Prospects for Peaceful Reform">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/04/ethiopia-contemplating-elections-and-prospects-peaceful-reform">Ethiopia: Contemplating Elections and the Prospects for Peaceful Reform</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, April 29, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Lidet Tadesse Shiferaw; &nbsp;Terrence Lyons; &nbsp;Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Ethiopia is approaching parliamentary elections on June 5. This will be the first vote since the process of reform launched in 2018 by Prime Minister Abiy Ahmed, and the stakes are extremely high. Elections to the next national parliament, the House of People&#039;s Representatives, may determine future decisions about the structure of the country and consolidate the ruling party’s power. While the short-term outlook for the vote is unlikely to change, the election may offer opportunities to support political dialogue which could sustain important reforms and decrease polarization. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/04/chad-president-debys-death-leaves-vacuum-volatile-region" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-04/20210422-chad_deby-ac.jpg?itok=9bk2ZobP" alt="Chad: President Déby’s Death Leaves Vacuum in Volatile Region ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/04/chad-president-debys-death-leaves-vacuum-volatile-region">Chad: President Déby’s Death Leaves Vacuum in Volatile Region </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, April 22, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Kamissa Camara; &nbsp;Jérôme Tubiana  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The sudden violent death of Idriss Déby, the leader of Chad since 1990, throws the central African country into uncertainty. During a visit to the military frontline, Déby was allegedly killed in fighting in the country’s Lake Chad region, just days after the uncompetitive April 11 presidential elections in which he was re-elected for a sixth term. As USIP’s Kamissa Camara and researcher Jérôme Tubiana explain, Déby’s death does not change the structural deficiencies of the Chadian state. At the same time, Déby’s death leaves the West without a long-time ally in counterterrorism in the greater Sahel and Lake Chad Basin, and how the transition is managed has implications for the wider region, too.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-bfa8b1950fe1262e61453b06ee101abadad75f2dde48b5756ab4565f348cce2e">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/04/south-sudans-people-have-spoken-peace-anyone-listening" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-04/20210416-nyt-sub_south_sudan_19-ac.jpg?itok=jkYYEWgi" alt="South Sudan’s people have spoken on peace. Is anyone listening?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/04/south-sudans-people-have-spoken-peace-anyone-listening">South Sudan’s people have spoken on peace. Is anyone listening?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, April 16, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ola Mohajer; &nbsp;David Deng  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The United States played a key role in the emergence of South Sudan as an independent state 10 years ago. Yet today, U.S. policy toward the country is insufficient to address the continued violence or promote sustainable peace. Even so, it is not too late for U.S. policymakers to embark upon a renewed push for peace. To move forward, they should listen to what South Sudan’s people said in the recently concluded National Dialogue and incorporate its recommendations in diplomatic, humanitarian and development strategies for the country.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/04/conflict-and-crisis-south-sudans-equatoria" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-04/20200414-sr_493_boswell_cover-sr.jpg?itok=VyhbTvUF" alt="Conflict and Crisis in South Sudan’s Equatoria">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/04/conflict-and-crisis-south-sudans-equatoria">Conflict and Crisis in South Sudan’s Equatoria</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, April 14, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Alan Boswell  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>South Sudan’s civil war expanded into Equatoria, the country’s southernmost region, in 2016, forcing hundreds of thousands to flee into neighboring Uganda in what has been called Africa’s largest refugee exodus since the 1994 Rwandan genocide. Equatoria is now the last major hot spot in the civil war. If lasting peace is to come to South Sudan, writes Alan Boswell, it will require a peace effort that more fully reckons with the long-held grievances of Equatorians.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=26#filters">Special Report</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/03/south-sudan-peace-process-archive-window-mediation" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-03/20210329-nyt-south_sudan_independence_6-ac.jpg?itok=6-JcTFOa" alt="The South Sudan Peace Process Archive: A Window into Mediation">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/03/south-sudan-peace-process-archive-window-mediation">The South Sudan Peace Process Archive: A Window into Mediation</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, March 29, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Zach Vertin; &nbsp;Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As part of its commitment to learning from peace processes, the U.S. Institute of Peace is pleased to launch the South Sudan Peace Process Archive, which aims to provide South Sudanese citizens, mediators, policymakers, academics and other interested readers a window into the 2013-2015 negotiations that attempted to end the conflict that began in South Sudan in late 2013. Documents for this archive were first assembled and organized in 2016. Now, archive curators and former peace process advisers Zach Vertin and Aly Verjee discuss their motivations for assembling and organizing the documents and what they hope the archive can contribute to future peace processes.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/03/despite-post-election-violence-niger-achieves-democratic-breakthrough" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-03/20210312-wc-niamey_flood_2013-ac.jpg?itok=4657jXMp" alt="Despite Post-Election Violence, Niger Achieves Democratic Breakthrough ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/03/despite-post-election-violence-niger-achieves-democratic-breakthrough">Despite Post-Election Violence, Niger Achieves Democratic Breakthrough </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, March 12, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Nourdine Harouna Abdou  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Niger’s presidential election has ushered in the West African nation’s first-ever democratic transition of power. As some international observers have heralded the success of these elections, accusations of irregularities have led to massive protests and government repression, including a 10-day internet shutdown. Hundreds of people have been arrested in the capital, Niamey, while police have clashed with protesters in several other cities. USIP’s Nourdine Harouna Abdou explains what happened in the first- and second-round votes and what the elections mean for peace and security in Niger. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/03/new-evidence-build-peace-include-women-start" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-03/20210311-nyt-myanmar_women_5-ac.jpg?itok=AfVE_9Fz" alt="New Evidence: To Build Peace, Include Women from the Start">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/03/new-evidence-build-peace-include-women-start">New Evidence: To Build Peace, Include Women from the Start</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, March 11, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Veronique Dudouet; &nbsp;Andreas Schädel  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>In the 20 years since governments declared it imperative to include women’s groups and their demands in peace processes, experience and research continue to show that this principle strengthens peace agreements and helps prevent wars from re-igniting. Yet our inclusion of women has been incomplete and, in some ways, poorly informed. Now a study of recent peace processes in Colombia, Mali, Afghanistan and Myanmar offers new guidance on how to shape women’s roles. A critical lesson is that we must ensure this inclusion from the start.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/03/mobilization-negotiation-and-transition-burkina-faso" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-03/sr_491-mobilization_negotiation_and_transition_in_burkina_faso-feature.jpg?itok=xCj5Y1Zq" alt="Mobilization, Negotiation, and Transition in Burkina Faso">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/03/mobilization-negotiation-and-transition-burkina-faso">Mobilization, Negotiation, and Transition in Burkina Faso</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, March 2, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Eloïse Bertrand  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>In October 2014, a massive popular uprising unseated Burkina Faso’s long-time president, Blaise Compaoré, and drove a civilian-led transition that culminated in free and fair elections in November 2015. This report shows the importance of the national culture of dialogue and consensus and the benefit of a vast, resilient network across negotiating groups. Although violence in the country has since increased, lessons from Burkina Faso’s transition can inform the dynamics of popular mobilization, negotiations, and prospects for long-term peace and democracy in other settings.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=26#filters">Special Report</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/03/south-sudan-10-states-32-states-and-back-again" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-03/20210301-nyt-ssudan_babies_4-ac.jpg?itok=HeAVFDbG" alt="South Sudan: From 10 States to 32 States and Back Again">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/03/south-sudan-10-states-32-states-and-back-again">South Sudan: From 10 States to 32 States and Back Again</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, March 1, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Matthew Pritchard; &nbsp;Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Last year, South Sudan reintroduced 10 subnational states in South Sudan, in place of the 32 states controversially created in 2017. Far from being an obscure matter of administrative organization, the initial, dramatic redivision of territory in the midst of protracted violence and large-scale displacement had a significant impact on representation, as well as social, economic, and political relations throughout the country. In 2018-19, researchers commissioned by USIP sought to better understand the decision-making process behind the creation of the 32 states in South Sudan. Researchers Matthew Pritchard and Aly Verjee discuss their findings in light of current developments. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/02/what-does-sudans-new-cabinet-mean-its-transition" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-02/20210208-nyt-sudan_personal_freedoms-ac.jpg?itok=UL8s4t5-" alt="What Does Sudan’s New Cabinet Mean for its Transition?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/02/what-does-sudans-new-cabinet-mean-its-transition">What Does Sudan’s New Cabinet Mean for its Transition?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, February 8, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Joseph Tucker   </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The announcement on February 8 of a new Cabinet in Khartoum—the product of a peace accord signed by Sudan’s transitional government with several armed groups in October 2020 through a deal brokered by South Sudan—offers hope that the broader inclusion of political leaders can help address Sudan’s pressing challenges and create peace dividends. Unfortunately, the lengthy process of selecting new Cabinet members revealed additional fractures among both signatories to the peace deal and civilian political elements that seemingly offer competing visions for the transition and beyond.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/01/could-national-dialogue-solve-ethiopias-political-crisis" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-01/20210119-nyt-ethiopia_sudan_refugees_8-ac.jpg?itok=0KHT4--7" alt="Could a National Dialogue Solve Ethiopia’s Political Crisis?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/01/could-national-dialogue-solve-ethiopias-political-crisis">Could a National Dialogue Solve Ethiopia’s Political Crisis?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, January 19, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Emebet Getachew; &nbsp;Mehari Taddele Maru; &nbsp;Yohannes Gedamu  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>While the recent conflict in Tigray renewed international focus on Ethiopia, more challenges lie ahead, including elections now scheduled for June 5. The state of Ethiopia’s political transition is contested, and the country remains polarized. However, as Ethiopian scholars Emebet Getachew, Mehari Taddele Maru, and Yohannes Gedamu discuss, a national dialogue process may have the potential to address the country’s dilemmas.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2021/01/ethiopias-worsening-crisis-threatens-regional-middle-east-security" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2021-01/20200113-nyt-ethiopia_sudan_refugees_9-ac.jpg?itok=5tAwSDIh" alt="Ethiopia’s Worsening Crisis Threatens Regional, Middle East Security">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2021/01/ethiopias-worsening-crisis-threatens-regional-middle-east-security">Ethiopia’s Worsening Crisis Threatens Regional, Middle East Security</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, January 13, 2021</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Payton Knopf; &nbsp;Jeffrey Feltman  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The Gulf Arabs recognize a strategic reality that has eluded the stove-piped U.S. foreign and security policy bureaucracy for too long: The Horn of Africa is an integral part of the Middle East’s security landscape, and increasingly so. No country demonstrates this more clearly than Ethiopia. That country’s escalating internal crises pose an increasingly grave threat not only to the country’s citizens but to international peace and security and to the interests of the United States and its partners in the Middle East, principally Egypt, Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates (UAE).</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

  <section class="page__main section -summary-spacing">
    
            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-a5369eeec703aa7a2f170a8dc2794d3d4b49bfca21b032c69b4171fbc8f59d66">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/12/unfolding-conflict-ethiopia" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-12/20201203-stigant_testimony_capitol-testimony.jpg?itok=Z1VodGVc" alt="The Unfolding Conflict in Ethiopia">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/12/unfolding-conflict-ethiopia">The Unfolding Conflict in Ethiopia</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, December 3, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Susan Stigant, director of Africa Programs at the U.S. Institute of Peace, testified on December 3, 2020 at the House Foreign Affairs Subcommittee on Africa, Global Health, Global Human Rights and International Organization&#039;s hearing on “The Unfolding Conflict in Ethiopia.” Her expert testimony as prepared is presented below.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=15#filters">Congressional Testimony</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/11/ethiopias-problems-will-not-end-military-victory" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-11/20201124-voting-queue-in-addis-ababa-flickr-ac.jpg?itok=e5ghDaeI" alt="Ethiopia’s Problems Will Not End with a Military Victory">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/11/ethiopias-problems-will-not-end-military-victory">Ethiopia’s Problems Will Not End with a Military Victory</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, November 24, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As violence continues over control of the northern Ethiopian region of Tigray, Ethiopia’s future remains unsettled, even if the conflict ends soon. Achieving the federal government’s security objectives in Tigray is unlikely to resolve both new and entrenched political challenges, and already delayed national elections, now expected in 2021, may prove a severe test of Ethiopia’s political order, and consequently affect broader regional stability. Reconciling the electoral process with efforts for reconciliation and national dialogue is now even more imperative.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/11/susan-stigant-ethiopias-escalating-civil-conflict" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Susan Stigant on Ethiopia’s Escalating Civil Conflict">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/11/susan-stigant-ethiopias-escalating-civil-conflict">Susan Stigant on Ethiopia’s Escalating Civil Conflict</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, November 19, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As rising violence in Ethiopia threatens to pull neighboring Eritrea into the fray, USIP’s Susan Stigant says, “There is a real need for some external, independent investigator to help diffuse some of that escalation” and look into disturbing reports of human rights violations stemming from the conflict.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/11/what-ethiopias-brewing-conflict-means-country-and-region" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-11/20201112-nyt-egypt_ethiopia_dam_4-ac.jpg?itok=H2trUF0f" alt="What Ethiopia’s Brewing Conflict Means for the Country—and the Region">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/11/what-ethiopias-brewing-conflict-means-country-and-region">What Ethiopia’s Brewing Conflict Means for the Country—and the Region</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, November 12, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee; &nbsp;Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Violent conflict between the federal government of Ethiopia and the federal state of Tigray, in the country’s north, began November 4 and quickly escalated. USIP’s Aly Verjee and Susan Stigant discuss the crisis and identify what could be done to avoid further violent conflict in east Africa’s most populous country.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/11/niger-foreign-security-interests-undermine-stability-what-can-be-done" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-11/20201104-nyt-us_military_africa-ac.jpg?itok=BPKOLc5k" alt="In Niger, Foreign Security Interests Undermine Stability—What Can Be Done?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/11/niger-foreign-security-interests-undermine-stability-what-can-be-done">In Niger, Foreign Security Interests Undermine Stability—What Can Be Done?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, November 4, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Emily Cole; &nbsp;Allison Grossman  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Over the past decade, the United States, France, and the European Unionhave drastically increased security assistance to countries in the Sahel region. They have done so to address two perceived transnational threats—violent extremism and mass migration to Europe—but have often neglected Sahel countries’ own interests and long-term stability. Nowhere is this more apparent than in Niger, the world’s poorest country. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/10/susan-stigant-sudans-latest-peace-agreement" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Susan Stigant on Sudan’s Latest Peace Agreement">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/10/susan-stigant-sudans-latest-peace-agreement">Susan Stigant on Sudan’s Latest Peace Agreement</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, October 8, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Sudan’s transitional government has signed a peace agreement to end a number of long-standing conflicts and civil wars. USIP’s Susan Stigant says this is a positive sign for democratic progress, as “one of the promises of the revolution was to seek peace,” but cautioned that the real “work only begins once the ink is on the paper.”</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/09/normalizing-sudan-israel-relations-now-dangerous-game" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-09/20200924-nyt-sudan_conflict_2-ac.jpg?itok=L-6qamYv" alt="Normalizing Sudan-Israel Relations Now is a Dangerous Game">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/09/normalizing-sudan-israel-relations-now-dangerous-game">Normalizing Sudan-Israel Relations Now is a Dangerous Game</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, September 24, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Payton Knopf; &nbsp;Jeffrey Feltman  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>With the UAE and Bahrain having joined Egypt and Jordan in declaring peace with Israel, those asking “who’s next?” often look enthusiastically westward, toward Khartoum. Adding new chapters to the Abraham Accords is in the U.S. interest, but so is a successful transition in Sudan. And the sequence of these steps is critical. A unified Sudanese government with a popular mandate will be better able to forge a warm and sustainable peace with Israel, whereas a rushed Israeli-Sudanese agreement has the potential to unravel Sudan’s transition and generate renewed support for Sudan’s Islamists and their foreign backers.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/09/how-international-security-support-contributed-malis-coup" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-09/20200921-nyt-france_sahel_14-ac.jpg?itok=7ZEUK5kG" alt="How International Security Support Contributed to Mali’s Coup">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/09/how-international-security-support-contributed-malis-coup">How International Security Support Contributed to Mali’s Coup</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, September 21, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Ena Dion; &nbsp;Emily Cole  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Since a 2012 coup, Mali has received significant security assistance from United States, France, the European Union and other foreign donors to address violent extremism and insurgency and help stabilize the country. In the wake of the August military coup, it is clear that strategy has backfired—and that, in fact, the failure of international security sector assistance to prioritize governance likely contributed to the conditions that led to the coup.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/09/malis-coup-harbinger-hope-or-uncertainty" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-09/20200910-nyt-mali_conflict_8-ac.jpg?itok=dG094lM8" alt="Mali’s Coup: Harbinger of Hope or Uncertainty">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/09/malis-coup-harbinger-hope-or-uncertainty">Mali’s Coup: Harbinger of Hope or Uncertainty</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, September 10, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Anushka Bose; &nbsp;Jonathan Pinckney, Ph.D.  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Last year was one of the most dramatic years of nonviolent action in recent memory, with millions taking to the streets to push for greater economic equality, democratic representation, and social justice. Some of the most dramatic uprisings took place in Africa, where longstanding repressive political regimes were forced from power in Sudan and Algeria, and protests over fuel prices in Zimbabwe led to a government crackdown. The recent almost entirely bloodless coup Mali, in which soldiers abducted President Ibrahim Boubacar Keita and forced him to resign capped a similar uprising, but is complicated by the role of the military in the president’s ouster and the COVID-19 pandemic.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/08/five-things-know-about-malis-coup" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-08/20200827-nyt-mali_france_conflict_10-ac.jpg?itok=q3y0Bo76" alt="Five Things to Know About Mali’s Coup">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/08/five-things-know-about-malis-coup">Five Things to Know About Mali’s Coup</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 27, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Emily Fornof; &nbsp;Emily Cole  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>On August 18, rising tensions to boiled over into a mutiny, leading to the resignation of President Ibrahim Boubacar Keita. This dramatic chain of events followed three months of protests, calling for Keita’s resignation. As the country grapples with an intractable insurgency and eight years of instability, anger over the government’s failure to resolve conflict, respect democratic norms, and provide basic services pushed citizens and the military to their boiling point. What comes next in Mali over the coming months could have significant implications for the country’s democracy and on the stability of the Sahel.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>
