                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-6938b29ad9e6c784e944c0aaf58cd6f679a0ce5a5bd8c5ed49fd9872ec794ab9">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/08/susan-stigant-malis-military-coup" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Susan Stigant on Mali’s Military Coup">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/08/susan-stigant-malis-military-coup">Susan Stigant on Mali’s Military Coup</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, August 26, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>After months of public protests, a military coup has toppled Mali’s government. USIP’s Susan Stigant looks at the path forward, saying “there’s a real tension in trying to figure out how to restore that constitutional order without necessarily going back to the status quo prior to the coup.”</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/05/chinas-response-sudans-political-transition" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-05/20200508-sr_466-feature_sr.jpg?itok=tCeJX96D" alt="China’s Response to Sudan’s Political Transition">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/05/chinas-response-sudans-political-transition">China’s Response to Sudan’s Political Transition</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, May 8, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Laura Barber  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Sudan&#039;s decades-long economic relationship with China has almost always been dominated by oil. Yet this relationship has changed significantly in the past decade—first with the loss of oil reserves when South Sudan became an independent nation in 2011, and more recently due to the ouster of longtime ally President Omar al-Bashir. This report, based on interviews with policy officials, diplomats, industry and security experts, and others, examines China’s evolving commercial and political interests in this vital nation in the Horn of Africa.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=26#filters">Special Report</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/05/sudan-one-year-after-bashir" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-05/20200501-nyt-sudan_protests_4-ac.jpg?itok=odPxfs1y" alt="Sudan, One Year After Bashir">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/05/sudan-one-year-after-bashir">Sudan, One Year After Bashir</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, May 1, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Manal Taha; &nbsp;Payton Knopf; &nbsp;Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Dictator Omar al-Bashir, who ruled Sudan for nearly three decades, was overthrown in April 2019. After months of protests, negotiations led to a joint civilian-military transitional government to govern the country for a period of 39 months. However, Sudan’s political transition remains tenuous, and even before the coronavirus pandemic, the risks of failure were many. USIP’s Manal Taha, Payton Knopf, and Aly Verjee discuss the past year in Sudan and the need for further international support to shore up the transition.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/03/four-lessons-outbreaks-africa-age-coronavirus" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-03/20200330-nyt-congo_ebola_4-ac.jpg?itok=FETd9s_V" alt="Four Lessons from Outbreaks in Africa for the Age of Coronavirus">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/03/four-lessons-outbreaks-africa-age-coronavirus">Four Lessons from Outbreaks in Africa for the Age of Coronavirus</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, March 30, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As the coronavirus pandemic continues and new behavioral practices—from social distancing to avoiding handshakes and hugs—become expected norms overnight, there are crucial policy lessons to be learned from struggles against previous outbreaks of disease in Africa. Despite widespread poverty, weak infrastructure, and relatively few health professionals, there is an encouraging, long record of African countries—often with significant international assistance and cooperation—eventually managing to overcome dire health challenges. For non-African countries already facing large numbers of COVID-19 infections, as well as for African countries where the epidemic is now at an early stage, policymakers would do well to recall these four lessons of past epidemics—of both what to do and, perhaps almost as importantly, what not to do to confront this global threat.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/03/south-sudans-transition-citizens-perception-peace" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-03/20200319-sudan_ousted_leader_4-ac.jpg?itok=4RkadZsr" alt="South Sudan’s Transition: Citizens’ Perception of Peace">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/03/south-sudans-transition-citizens-perception-peace">South Sudan’s Transition: Citizens’ Perception of Peace</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, March 19, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Jan Pospisil; &nbsp;Oringa Christopher; &nbsp;Sophia Dawkins; &nbsp;David Deng  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Last month’s breakthrough between South Sudan’s government and its armed opposition on establishing a new transitional government represents a critical step toward ending the country’s civil war, a conflict that over the past six years has killed more than 400,000 people and displaced a third of the nation’s population of 12 million.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/03/amid-red-sea-rivalries-eritrea-plays-independence" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-03/20200311-eritrea-ac.jpg?itok=gvE_jQlg" alt="Amid Red Sea Rivalries, Eritrea Plays for Independence">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/03/amid-red-sea-rivalries-eritrea-plays-independence">Amid Red Sea Rivalries, Eritrea Plays for Independence</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, March 11, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Harry Verhoeven  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>When Eritrea’s president last month hosted the leaders of Ethiopia and Somalia to discuss “regional cooperation,” that initiative drew few global headlines. Still, Eritrea’s move should be noted by policymakers and others working for stability in the Horn of Africa and the Red Sea region. For years, President Isaias Afwerki’s disdain for multilateral forums such as the African Union, and his strained relations with many governments in the region, have contributed to caricatures of Eritrea as the “North Korea of Africa.” But his invitation for two neighbors to discuss a new regional bloc reflects an important factor in Eritrea’s foreign policy: its efforts to preserve its independence in a fast-evolving geopolitical environment.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/02/payton-knopf-whats-ahead-horn-africa-2020" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Payton Knopf on What’s Ahead for the Horn of Africa in 2020">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/02/payton-knopf-whats-ahead-horn-africa-2020">Payton Knopf on What’s Ahead for the Horn of Africa in 2020</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, February 13, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Payton Knopf  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>As Arab Gulf states and Turkey ramp up their competition for influence in the Horn of Africa, USIP’s Payton Knopf says the increased attention “has tended to exacerbate some of the internal tensions and political insecurities” in Ethiopia and Sudan—two states undergoing democratic transitions vital for regional stability.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2020/02/what-happens-when-sudan-removed-us-terror-list" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2020-02/20200206-nyt-2019_yearend_news_236-ac.jpg?itok=RmZvknjC" alt="What Happens When Sudan is Removed from the U.S. Terror List?">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2020/02/what-happens-when-sudan-removed-us-terror-list">What Happens When Sudan is Removed from the U.S. Terror List?</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, February 6, 2020</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Hilary Mossberg; &nbsp;John Prendergast   </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Its been nearly a year since Sudan’s longtime dictator, Omar al-Bashir, was ousted from power. As the country moves to transition to democracy, its civilian government and Sudanese civil society have called on the U.S. government to remove Khartoum from the State Sponsors of Terrorism (SST) list. The Sentry’s Hillary Mossberg and John Prendergast recently argued that although delist-ing is an important for Sudan’s transition, it is just one of multiple steps needed—from both the U.S. and Sudan—in order for pro-democracy forces to achieve their goals. Mossberg and Prender-gast explain what the actual impact of delisting would be and what Sudan’s government can do to get there. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/11/nobel-laureate-abiy-ahmeds-next-peacebuilding-project-should-be-home" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-11/20191114-wikimedia_abiy-ac.jpg?itok=L5Sf0dfq" alt="Nobel Laureate Abiy Ahmed’s Next Peacebuilding Project Should be at Home">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/11/nobel-laureate-abiy-ahmeds-next-peacebuilding-project-should-be-home">Nobel Laureate Abiy Ahmed’s Next Peacebuilding Project Should be at Home</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, November 14, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Ethiopian Prime Minister Abiy Ahmed has remained in the news in the weeks following his 2019 Nobel Peace Prize—but not for the reasons you’d expect. An estimated 86 people have died in violence sparked by an alleged assassination attempt against a prominent political opposition leader. This tragedy is symptomatic of Ethiopia’s fragile transition and demonstrates the urgency for Dr. Abiy to focus his energies at home to deliver a peaceful transition for the 105 million Ethiopians counting on his leadership. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/10/susan-stigant-ethiopias-nobel-peace-prize-winner" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Susan Stigant on Ethiopia’s Nobel Peace Prize Winner">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/10/susan-stigant-ethiopias-nobel-peace-prize-winner">Susan Stigant on Ethiopia’s Nobel Peace Prize Winner</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, October 16, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Last week, Ethiopian Prime Minister Abiy Ahmed was awarded the Nobel Peace Prize for his diplomatic engagement with neighboring Eritrea and initiating a host of domestic reforms. USIP’s Susan Stigant explains how the award shines a light on his accomplishments and “sets an expectation that he will continue to provide that leadership going forward.&quot;</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

            <section class="container row -exact">
                  <div class="span9">
            
            <div class="views-element-container"><div class="js-view-dom-id-8014b0aa211ad31f6faa33879d392caffbbfe9cc9c0ab9cf082cd389caa7da00">
  
  
  

  
  
  

  



  





    
      
    
    
                        <article class="summary f">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/10/south-sudan-hope-best-plan-worst" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-10/20191007-president_of_south_sudan_meets_opposition_leader_in_juba-820570-un-photo-ac.jpg?itok=ibgCWUYD" alt="South Sudan: Hope for the Best, Plan for the Worst">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/10/south-sudan-hope-best-plan-worst">South Sudan: Hope for the Best, Plan for the Worst</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, October 7, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    David Deng; &nbsp;Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>With little more than a month left before a new transitional government is set to assume power in South Sudan, efforts to keep the latest peace agreement on track are becoming more urgent, even as most key pre-transition deadlines have been missed and the political will of the belligerents remains in doubt. Given these circumstances, efforts to support the current process remain vitally necessary and thorough planning for the worst-case scenarios is also desperately needed in case South Sudan’s fragile peace collapses.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/09/conflict-prevention-kenya-combating-corruption-through-nonviolent-action" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-09/20190925-sr-456-feature-sr.jpg?itok=SXKnKATJ" alt="Conflict Prevention in Kenya: Combating Corruption through Nonviolent Action">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/09/conflict-prevention-kenya-combating-corruption-through-nonviolent-action">Conflict Prevention in Kenya: Combating Corruption through Nonviolent Action</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Wednesday, September 25, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Tabatha Thompson; &nbsp;Hussein Khalid   </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The relationship between corruption and violent conflict is complex and significant. Corruption affects access to basic services, contributes to resource scarcity, and fuels organized crime. It was included on a European Commission checklist for the root causes of conflict, and it was cited as a potential driver of extremism in the 2019 report of the Task Force on Extremism in Fragile States. Focusing on several social movements in Kenya, this report reviews the efforts of collective civic action to combat corruption and advance transparency, accountability, and good governance.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=26#filters">Special Report</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/09/ethiopias-experiment-reconciliation" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-09/20190923-nyt-ethiopia_election_7-ac.jpg?itok=Kt3726dX" alt="Ethiopia’s Experiment in Reconciliation ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/09/ethiopias-experiment-reconciliation">Ethiopia’s Experiment in Reconciliation </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Monday, September 23, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Solomon Ayele Dersso   </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>In February 2019, the Ethiopian parliament adopted a landmark proclamation establishing a national reconciliation commission, the first-ever such institution in Ethiopia. Six months on, the commission has developed a three-year plan and begun consultations. But the body was formed without broad-based political consensus regarding its mandate, so has yet to win the critical trust of Ethiopia’s many social and political groups. Dr. Solomon Ayele Dersso discusses the mandate of this body, the challenges ahead, and how the commission could help build peace in Africa’s second most populous country.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/08/ceasefire-monitoring-south-sudan-2014-2019-very-ugly-mission" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-08/20190830-pw_150-feature-pw.jpg?itok=hB7D1QK2" alt="Ceasefire Monitoring in South Sudan 2014–2019: “A Very Ugly Mission”">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/08/ceasefire-monitoring-south-sudan-2014-2019-very-ugly-mission">Ceasefire Monitoring in South Sudan 2014–2019: “A Very Ugly Mission”</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, August 30, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>More than five years after South Sudan’s first ceasefire agreement, ceasefire monitors are still on the ground. The hope was that their work would help overcome the mistrust between rival factions, halt ongoing violence, and deter further violations. Drawing on interviews with monitors, combatants, politicians, civil society representatives, diplomats, peacekeepers, and others, this report examines the history of ceasefire monitoring in South Sudan and offers recommendations for donors supporting future monitoring processes in South Sudan and elsewhere.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=27#filters">Peaceworks</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/08/year-after-ethiopia-eritrea-peace-deal-what-impact" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-08/20190829-nyt-eritrea_progress_18-ac.jpg?itok=pkwx8VTb" alt="A Year After the Ethiopia-Eritrea Peace Deal, What Is the Impact? ">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/08/year-after-ethiopia-eritrea-peace-deal-what-impact">A Year After the Ethiopia-Eritrea Peace Deal, What Is the Impact? </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, August 29, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Susan Stigant; &nbsp;Michael V. Phelan  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Ethiopia and Eritrea signed a peace agreement just over a year ago to end two decades of a “frozen war.” The accord, which resolved a seemingly intractable border dispute after Ethiopian Prime Minister Abiy Ahmed took office and accepted an independent commission’s 2002 boundary decision, was greeted with tremendous optimism in both countries and by international observers. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/08/southern-ethiopia-trouble-brews-sidama" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-08/20190806-wikimedia-sidama_ethiopia-ac.jpg?itok=mgn0Cb7M" alt="In Southern Ethiopia, Trouble Brews in Sidama">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/08/southern-ethiopia-trouble-brews-sidama">In Southern Ethiopia, Trouble Brews in Sidama</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, August 6, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The southern Ethiopian area of Sidama is famous for its coffee. But amid the beans, bitterness lingers. More than 50 people were killed in recent violence, as Ethiopia struggles with demands for the creation of a new Sidama ethnic federal state—a right explicitly permitted by the national constitution. USIP’s Aly Verjee discusses the implications of this latest challenge to peace in Africa’s second most populous country.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/06/sudan-remains-stalemate-after-militarys-crackdown" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-06/20190620-nyt-sudan_explainer-ac.jpg?itok=BgwVqBqw" alt="Sudan Remains at a Stalemate After the Military’s Crackdown">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/06/sudan-remains-stalemate-after-militarys-crackdown">Sudan Remains at a Stalemate After the Military’s Crackdown</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, June 20, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Elizabeth Murray  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>It’s been over two months since Sudan’s longtime dictator, Omar al-Bashir, was overthrown by the country’s military following months of popular protests. On June 3, the Transitional Military Council (TMC)—which has been ruling since Bashir’s ouster—escalated its lethal crackdown on peaceful protesters in Khartoum and other cities. The protesters say that their demand is the same as before—a transition to civilian rule—but that they will not negotiate with the TMC unless it first meets certain conditions. What’s happening in Sudan? When will negotiations on the country’s transition resume? How can the international community help? USIP’s Elizabeth Murray discusses the latest on the situation in Sudan. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/06/religious-landscape-south-sudan-challenges-and-opportunities-engagement" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-06/capture.jpg?itok=R3joBye5" alt="The Religious Landscape in South Sudan: Challenges and Opportunities for Engagement">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/06/religious-landscape-south-sudan-challenges-and-opportunities-engagement">The Religious Landscape in South Sudan: Challenges and Opportunities for Engagement</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, June 20, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Jacqueline Wilson  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>Since the beginning of South Sudan&#039;s civil war in 2013, the country&#039;s religious actors have sought to play an active role in turning the tide from war and violence to peace and reconciliation. Drawing on interviews, focus groups, and consultations, this report maps the religious landscape of South Sudan and showcases the legitimate and influential religious actors and institutions, highlights challenges impeding their peace work, and provides recommendations for policymakers and practitioners to better engage with religious actors for peace.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=27#filters">Peaceworks</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/06/ethiopia-former-us-diplomats-see-promise-reform" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-06/20190613-changing_ethiopia-ac.jpg?itok=ujP5feiI" alt="In Ethiopia, Former U.S. Diplomats See Promise in Reform">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/06/ethiopia-former-us-diplomats-see-promise-reform">In Ethiopia, Former U.S. Diplomats See Promise in Reform</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, June 13, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Fred Strasser  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>In Ethiopia, political prisoners are free and the security services revamped. Women now comprise half the cabinet, and serve as ceremonial head of state, chief justice, and chair of the electoral commission. Significant steps have been taken toward resolving a 20-year conflict with neighboring Eritrea and reforms to unleash the economy—already one of Africa’s fastest growing—are ostensibly on the way. Elections are slated for next year. Under Abiy Ahmed, the nation’s popular new prime minister, Ethiopia is changing in ways long desired by American policymakers, agreed four former U.S. ambassadors to the country. Yet the most the U.S. is likely to do is offer encouragement and a bit of support, they said. </p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

  <div class="summary__text " >

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/05/how-address-africas-staggering-displacement-crisis">How to Address Africa’s ‘Staggering’ Displacement Crisis </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 30, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Anthony Navone  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              <p>Fifty years after the Organization of African Unity Convention on Refugees and 10 years after the Kampala Convention on Internally Displaced Persons (IDPs), Africa faces a crisis of forced displacemen</p>
    
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>

                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/05/payton-knopf-stakes-sudan" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Payton Knopf on the Stakes in Sudan">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/05/payton-knopf-stakes-sudan">Payton Knopf on the Stakes in Sudan</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 23, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Payton Knopf  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>What’s at stake in Sudan as tense negotiations between the Transitional Military Council and protesters continue? “We need to see a swift transition to civilian-led rule,” says Payton Knopf. “Otherwise I’m afraid what will result is increased instability … or potentially a catastrophic failure of the state.” 

</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/05/amid-rising-sahel-violence-burkina-faso-builds-response" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-05/20190516-saaba-ac.jpg?itok=bW0fHi8D" alt="Amid Rising Sahel Violence, Burkina Faso Builds a Response">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/05/amid-rising-sahel-violence-burkina-faso-builds-response">Amid Rising Sahel Violence, Burkina Faso Builds a Response</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 16, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    James Rupert  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>A perfect storm of violence is breaking upon Africa’s Sahel. Since late 2018, communal conflicts—many over access to food, water or productive land—have produced thousands of deadly attacks. Across the region, nearly 4,800 people died in conflicts from November to March, according to the violence-monitoring group ACLED. The greatest surge in bloodshed is in Burkina Faso, where communal militias or religious extremists killed 500 people over five months. But amid the dire headlines, governments and civic groups in Burkina Faso and other Sahel countries cite progress in stabilizing communities with a basic step that simply has seldom been undertaken: broad, local dialogues among community groups, police forces and officials. Community leaders and government officials say they are now expanding those dialogues to improve national security policies to help counter the tide of violence.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/05/thomas-hill-upheaval-north-africa" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2018-02/usip-sirius-on-peace-podcast-logo.jpg?itok=A2q3ZZBp" alt="Thomas Hill on the Upheaval in North Africa">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/05/thomas-hill-upheaval-north-africa">Thomas Hill on the Upheaval in North Africa</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 2, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Thomas M. Hill  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>From Algeria to Libya and beyond, North Africa has been roiled by unrest in recent months. USIP’s Thomas Hill says at its core this turmoil is the result of “governments having not performed to the degree that they [the people] want or need them to” and discusses how it impacts U.S. interests.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=11#filters">Podcast</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

    




      
    
    
                        <article class="summary">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/05/chad-and-darfur-after-bashir" class="summary__image">
    <img src="https://www.usip.org/sites/default/files/styles/summary_image/public/2019-05/20190502-nyt-reportajes_polgreen_chad2_feb22-ac.jpg?itok=d1H1Inj9" alt="Chad, and Darfur, After Bashir">
  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/05/chad-and-darfur-after-bashir">Chad, and Darfur, After Bashir</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Thursday, May 2, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Jérôme Tubiana; &nbsp;Aly Verjee  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>The politics of the Central African nation of Chad are closely connected with those of Sudan, most prominently because of Darfur, the vast and troubled Sudanese region which borders Chad to the east. The recent fall of Sudan’s president Omar al-Bashir—in power since 1989—raises questions about the future of Chad’s president and U.S. ally, Idriss Déby, beset by similar governance challenges and in power since 1990. Jérôme Tubiana, co-author of a 2017 USIP report on Chad, and USIP’s Aly Verjee discuss the implications of political change in Sudan for Chad.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
      
      
    
</div>


      </article>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/04/year-change-ethiopia">A Year of Change in Ethiopia</a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Tuesday, April 2, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Aly Verjee; &nbsp;Payton Knopf  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>A year ago today, 42-year old reformist politician Abiy Ahmed became prime minister of Ethiopia. Abiy came to power during a deep political crisis with widespread grievances across this country of over 105 million people, and quickly began to enact political reforms. USIP’s Aly Verjee and Payton Knopf discuss Abiy’s year as prime minister and identify the challenges that lie ahead for eastern Africa’s most populous country.</p>

                        <article class="summary l">
        
    
      <a  href="/publications/2019/02/south-sudan-nonviolent-action-essential-building-peace" class="summary__image">

  </a>

  <div class="summary__text " >
  <header class="summary__header">

    <h3 class="summary__heading"><a  href="/publications/2019/02/south-sudan-nonviolent-action-essential-building-peace">In South Sudan, Nonviolent Action is Essential to Building Peace </a></h3>

                  <p class="meta">
  <span class="published-date">Friday, February 22, 2019</span>

</p>
                    <p class="meta">    <p class="meta publication-by-line">
    <strong>By: </strong>

    Maria J. Stephan ; &nbsp;Nicholas Zaremba  </p>
  </p>
      
      
    

  </header>

              

      <p>On September 12 of last year, South Sudan’s president, Salva Kiir, signed the Revitalized Agreement on the Resolution of Conflict in South Sudan (R-ARCSS) with South Sudan People Liberation Movement in Opposition chairman Dr. Riek Machar and several other armed groups. Meanwhile, South Sudanese civil society has sought to further advance the country’s peace process through coordinated, strategic nonviolent actions and campaigns.</p>
  


        
    
              <p class="meta"><strong>Type: </strong> 

      <a href="/research-analysis?publication_type%5B0%5D=12#filters">Analysis</a>  
</p>
